# Autoimmune Complications of Immune Checkpoint Inhibitors for Cancer

LAURA C. CAPPELLI, AMI A. SHAH, AND CLIFTON O. BINGHAM, III

KEY POINTS

> Immune checkpoint inhibitors, used for the treatment of cancer, can

> cause a wide variety of inflammatory syndromes known as immune-

> related adverse events (irAEs), including some with rheumatic

> phenotypes.

> The precise mechanisms underlying irAEs are not well defined and may

> be different for different types of events.

> Inflammatory arthritis is the most common irAE encountered by

> rheumatologists and can have a heterogeneous presentation in

> terms of severity, time to onset, and affected joints.

> Treatment for rheumatic irAEs usually starts with corticosteroids, but

> other forms of immunosuppression have a role in patients with

> steroid nonresponse, severe disease, or who are unable to wean or

> tolerate steroids.

> Patients with pre-existing autoimmune disease treated with immune

> checkpoint inhibitors may have a flare of their underlying disease,

> but mostly tolerate therapy without a need to discontinue.

## Introduction

Immunotherapy has proven paradigm shifting for the field of
oncology. The idea that activating patients’ immune systems could
treat their cancers dates back to surgeon William Coley, who
administered a bacterial toxin to patients with inoperable tumors,
causing tumor regression in some.
This concept laid relatively
dormant for the first half of the 20th century but found enthusiasm again with successes of high-dose interleukin (IL)-2 infusions
for treatment of melanoma in the 1980s.
In the last two decades,
the field has grown exponentially, with immune checkpoint
inhibitors (ICIs) being the most commonly used cancer immunotherapy currently. In this chapter we focus primarily on issues
related to ICIs of relevance to the rheumatology community, that
is, rheumatic immune-related adverse events (irAEs), and use of
ICIs for cancer treatment in patients with pre-existing autoimmune disease. These topics are contextualized with a broader view
of ICIs and the full spectrum of irAEs.

### Immune Checkpoint Inhibitor Mechanism of Action

ICIs target inhibitory receptors and ligands on T cells, antigenpresenting cells, and/or tumor cells (
Fig. 130.1A, B
).
By blocking negative co-stimulatory signals, activation of the T cell can
occur relatively unchecked.
This is thought to invigorate immune
responses to the tumor that may have been dampened by chronic
antigen stimulation or by other factors in the tumor microenvironment. The effects of cytotoxic lymphocyte-associated protein
4 (CTLA-4) and programmed cell death 1/programmed death
ligand 1 (PD-1/PD-L1) blockade are not identical, and these
therapies are now thought to work through different mechanisms.
CTLA-4 inhibition lowers the threshold for expansion of activated
T cells, but recent data suggest killing of T regulatory cells may be a
key factor to the anti-tumor response.
PD-1 or PD-L1 blockade
can activate exhausted T cells but may also work through blocking
the inhibitory effects on dendritic cells and natural killer cells.
The
first ICI drug approved for use in the United States, ipilimumab,
targets CTLA-4 on T cells. Subsequently approved drugs have targeted either PD-1 (nivolumab, pembrolizumab, cemiplimab) or
its ligand PD-L1 (atezolizumab, durvalumab, avelumab). Many
other drugs are in development targeting related immune checkpoints, such as T cell immunoglobulin and mucin domain-3
(TIM3), CD137, and lymphocyte activation gene-3 (LAG-3)
(
Fig. 130.1C
). An anti-LAG-3 agent, relatlimab, was approved in
the United States in 2022 for use in combination with nivolumab
for treatment of melanoma. Drugs targeting other checkpoints are
being studied alone and in combination with existing ICIs.

### Other Types of Cancer Immunotherapy

ICIs, the focus of this chapter, are just one class of therapeutics that
utilizes the immune system for treatment of malignancies. Chimeric antigen receptor (CAR) T cells are a different type of cancer immunotherapy in which patients’ own T cells are genetically
engineered to recognize antigens on the tumor target. CAR T cells

recognizing CD19 were first approved for the treatment of refractory pre-B cell acute lymphoblastic leukemia and diffuse large B
cell lymphoma.
CAR T cells with a variety of targets are now being
trialed in adults for a range of hematologic malignancies,
with
attempts to move into solid tumors.
Cytokine release syndrome,
which can be severe and cause hypotension, has been a major side
effect; this has been managed primarily with the IL-6 receptor (IL6R) inhibitor tocilizumab and corticosteroids.
Neurotoxicity has
also been a rare but life-threatening issue with CAR T cells.
A
variety of cancer vaccines have also been in development over the
last several decades for use primarily in solid tumors with limited
clinical success.
Several clinical trials have combined cancer vaccines with ICIs hoping to increase responses.
IL-2 infusions were
previously used in cancers such as melanoma and renal cell carcinoma but have been mostly replaced by ICIs. Tumor-infiltrating

### Immune Checkpoint Inhibitors in Oncology

The first indication for ICIs was advanced-stage melanoma; ipilimumab was approved by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration
for this indication in 2011. Agents that block CTLA-4, PD-1, and
PD-L1, plus the combination of CTLA-4 and PD-1 blockade, are
now approved for a variety of tumor types in the United States
(
Table 130.1
). With combination CTLA-4 and PD-1 blockade in
advanced-stage melanoma, the 3-year survival rate is nearly 60%.
Advanced stage non–small cell lung cancer (NSCLC) is another
indication for ICI therapy and is a considerably more common
diagnosis than advanced-stage melanoma. Survival benefits are
more modest in NSCLC; combination therapy in patients with

high tumor mutational burden has a 1-year progression-free survival rate of 42.6%,
and nivolumab monotherapy in advanced
NSCLC has a 1-year progression-free survival rate of 19%.
Other approved indications include renal cell carcinoma,
Hodgkin’s lymphoma,
urothelial carcinoma,
advanced cutaneous
squamous cell carcinoma,
Merkel cell carcinoma,
any solid
tumor with microsatellite instability
(a genetic alteration that
leads to a high mutation rate), and several other tumor types. ICIs
are being used for earlier-stage disease and in an ever-increasing
number of tumor types. They are also being used in the adjuvant
setting as in high-risk melanoma that has been resected
and in
the neoadjuvant setting prior to surgery for NSCLC.

## Immune-Related Adverse Events

The main class of side effects due to ICIs, known as irAEs, are
related to excessive or off-target inflammation that causes nontumor tissue damage. That the general mechanism of toxicity
for ICIs is closely related to the desired mechanism of efficacy
(increased activity of the immune system) is unique among many
cancer therapies and potentially complicates treatment.

### Clinical Spectrum of Immune-Related Adverse Events

The spectrum of clinical presentations for irAEs ranges widely,
including the severity of events, temporal relationship to ICI exposure, and treatment strategies. There is also significant heterogeneity in prevalence, relationship to type of ICI, and relationship to
underlying tumor type. Nearly every organ system can be affected
by irAEs. The most common irAEs are dermatologic conditions,

> Indication(s)

> Melanoma

> Melanoma, NSCLC, RCC, refractory Hodgkin’s lymphoma, urothelial carcinoma,

> SCCHN, esophageal carcinoma, HCC, CRC with MSI-H, gastric cancer

> Melanoma, NSCLC, urothelial carcinoma, MSI-H solid tumors, SCCHN, HCC, TNBC,

> cervical cancer, esophageal cancer, gastric cancer, mesothelioma, Merkel cell

> carcinoma, primary mediastinal large B cell lymphoma

> Urothelial carcinoma, NSCLC, HCC, small cell lung cancer, melanoma

> Merkel cell carcinoma, urothelial carcinoma, RCC, gestational trophoblastic

> neoplasia

> NSCLC, small cell lung cancer, biliary tract cancer

> Advanced cutaneous squamous cell carcinoma, basal cell carcinoma, cervical

> cancer, NSCLC

> Melanoma, RCC, CRC with MSI-H, HCC, esophageal cancer, NSCLC, mesothelioma

> Melanoma

occurring in as many as 30% of ICI-treated patients, and hypothyroidism in up to 20%.
Rare but potentially severe irAEs include
myocarditis, occurring in less than 1% of patients with irAEs, and
neurologic toxicities such as Guillain-Barré syndrome, encephalitis, and myasthenia gravis.
Other irAEs include hepatitis,
hypophysitis (inflammation of the pituitary), colitis, pneumonitis,
pancreatitis, insulin-dependent diabetes, uveitis, peripheral neuropathy, and irAEs similar to classic rheumatologic diseases, which
are discussed in detail later in this chapter. Many irAEs range
widely in severity; both pneumonitis and colitis can be life threatening in some cases but can also be mild and self-limited. Similarly, rash may respond to topical therapy or can be severe, with
cases of Stevens-Johnson syndrome
and toxic epidermal necrolysis reported.
Some irAEs, such as colitis and rash, tend to develop
early in the treatment course, whereas others, like pneumonitis
and inflammatory arthritis (IA), have a variable time to onset.
Colitis and hypophysitis are more common with CTLA-4 inhibition.
Pneumonitis, hypothyroidism, and vitiligo, on the other
hand, are more common with PD-1 inhibition.
Patients with
melanoma are more likely to have dermatologic and gastrointestinal (GI) irAEs but less likely to have pneumonitis.

### Potential Mechanisms Underlying Immune- Related Adverse Events

The underlying mechanisms of irAEs are not well understood
across organ systems. Given the differences in clinical presentation and responses to certain immunosuppressive therapies, there
are likely multiple mechanisms contributing. The following is a
brief summary of potential mechanisms for irAEs and evidence
supporting those mechanisms.

• Cytokine-mediated inflammatory damage. Levels of serum
IL-17 both before ICI treatment and after 6 weeks of therapy
correlated with development of severe colitis.
Tumor necrosis
factor (TNF) inhibitors and IL-6 receptor inhibitors have been
effective treatments for irAEs such as colitis, pneumonitis, IA,
and polymyalgia rheumatica.
• Th-1 and Th-17 T cell-mediated damage. Th1-CD8+ T cells
are expanded in blood and synovial fluid of patients with ICIinduced IA.
More specifically, patients with ICI-induced IA
treated with combined anti-CTLA-4/PD-1 therapy demonstrate an enhanced Th17 signature.
• Expression of CTLA-4 or PD-1/PD-L1 on target tissue.
CTLA-4 is expressed on pituitary tissue, the target tissue leading to the irAE of hypophysitis.
Though hypophysitis is more
common with CTLA-4 inhibitors, it still occurs in patients on
anti-PD-1 or anti-PD-L1 drugs, thus cross-reactivity with tissue is not the only mechanism at play.
• Shared antigen recognition by T cells. T cell receptor sequencing from myocardium, skeletal muscle, and tumors from two
cases of ICI-related myocarditis showed that the T cell clone
most prevalent in heart muscle was also seen in amplified numbers in skeletal muscle and found in tumor tissue.
Development of vitiligo has been associated with a positive tumor
response to ICIs in melanoma,
which is thought to be due
to a common immune response against melanocytes in normal
skin and melanoma cells.
• Activation of pre-existing subclinical autoimmunity. Several
patients with ICI-induced myasthenia gravis have been found
to have pre-existing acetylcholine receptor antibodies.
Also,
two patients with IA who had anti-citrullinated peptide antibodies before ICI therapy developed clinically apparent IA
only after treatment.
• Perturbations of the microbiome. Alterations in GI or other
mucosal flora may also influence development of irAEs. For
example, a particular alteration in baseline colonic microbiome
(at the start of ICI therapy) is seen more often in patients who
develop colitis due to ICIs compared with other ICI-treated
patients.
This area is actively being investigated in melanoma
and NSCLC, in addition to how the microbiome may affect
tumor response to ICI therapy.
• Shared genetic predisposition to classic autoimmune diseases.
In insulin-dependent diabetes as an irAE, several patients
have been found to have high-risk human leukocyte antigen
(HLA) alleles that predispose for type 1 diabetes mellitus.
A pilot study in ICI-induced IA showed an increase in HLADRB1 shared epitope alleles (genetic risk factors in rheumatoid
arthritis [RA]) in patients with ICI-induced IA compared with
healthy controls.

### The Relationship Between Immune-Related Adverse Events and Tumor Response

Data have been published on a variety of tumor types indicating that developing an irAE may be a positive prognostic factor for tumor response to therapy. In a study of nivolumab for
melanoma, there was a higher response rate in those with irAEs
compared with those with no irAEs; the highest rate of tumor
regression (80%) was in those with grade 3 irAEs or higher.
In NSCLC, development of irAEs was associated with improved
survival outcomes for those with advanced or recurrent disease
treated with nivolumab.
In a pooled analysis of nonmelanoma
cancers treated with PD-1 inhibition, there were also better overall
response rates in those who developed irAEs.

## Rheumatologic Immune-Related Adverse Events

Immune-related adverse events can present similarly as many
classic rheumatologic diseases. There are important differences,
however, in clinical phenotype, autoantibodies, and treatment
modalities that in many instances differentiate rheumatologic
irAEs from their non-ICI-induced counterparts.

### Epidemiology

The prevalence of irAEs with rheumatologic phenotypes has not
been well characterized in ICI clinical trial data.
There are several reasons that likely explain this. First, musculoskeletal AEs
typically have a variety of codes in adverse event grading systems
for oncology trials that could be used to describe events that were
actually IA, polymyalgia rheumatica, or myositis (e.g., arthralgia,
arthritis, joint swelling, periarthritis, myalgia). Second, rheumatologic irAEs are rarely life threatening or severe by oncology adverse
event grading and are therefore less likely to be reported. Information on epidemiology comes primarily from single-center retrospective studies or from databases for drug adverse event reporting.
IA appears to be the most common rheumatic irAE, with estimates of prevalence ranging between 3% and 7% in retrospective
studies.
This may be an underestimate due to previous lack of
awareness of IA as a side effect of ICIs. Myositis occurs in less than
1% of ICI-treated patients.
The rates of giant cell arteritis, sicca
syndrome, other types of vasculitis, and scleroderma-like illness
due to ICI therapy have not been well defined.

### Inflammatory Arthritis

Inflammatory arthritis as a result of ICI therapy was first noted in
the rheumatology literature in 2015.
Since that time, there have
been numerous retrospective studies detailing clinical features and
response to treatment.
IA due to ICIs can have a clinically
heterogeneous presentation; patients typically present differently
than in RA, psoriatic arthritis, or other spondyloarthritis. In one
series, most patients presented with a large joint affected first, but
up to a third of patients had small joints of the fingers and wrists
initially affected.
Patients may also have an additive course where
they start with a mono- or oligoarthritis and develop into a polyarthritis. Significant tendon involvement with tenosynovitis and
enthesitis has been reported by several groups (
Fig. 130.2A
).
Reactive arthritis with concomitant urethritis and conjunctivitis
has also been described.
In the limited reports to date, all cases
presenting with ICI-related reactive arthritis also had colitis as a
preceding irAE. Patients can rapidly develop erosive disease within
months of symptom onset.
Additionally, inflammatory back and
neck pain have been described in a limited number of patients
(
Fig. 130.2B
).
Patients with ICI-induced IA have been primarily seronegative
for traditional autoantibodies including anti-nuclear antibodies,
rheumatoid factor (RF), and anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide antibodies (CCPs) with 90% seronegative in one systematic literature
review.
There are reports of patients seropositive for both RF
and CCP, some of whom had measurable autoantibodies in serum

collected before ICI therapy was started in the absence of arthritis symptoms.
Similarly, HLA-B27, the main genetic risk factor
for ankylosing spondylitis, has been negative in the majority of
patients tested. Inflammatory markers are elevated in the majority
of patients but not all. Those treated with combination therapy
appear to have higher inflammatory markers than those treated
with PD-1/PD-L1 monotherapy in one cohort of patients; those
with combination therapy are also more likely to have arthritis
that starts in the knee and to have a reactive arthritis phenotype.
Imaging results of ICI-induced IA with magnetic resonance
imaging (MRI) and ultrasound has been characterized. Patients
have a variety of findings including Doppler positive synovitis,
effusions, erosions, tenosynovitis, and enthesitis on musculoskeletal ultrasound.
Similar findings of synovitis, effusions, and erosions may also be seen on MRI.
An interesting study of MRI in
patients on ICI presenting with musculoskeletal pain showed that
B

> (left)

> and fat-suppressed contrast-enhanced

> Autoimmun Rev

> 17[10]:1040–1045, 2018.)

periarticular tissues may also be involved with myofasciitis seen
in addition to synovitis in several patients.
One study evaluated
computed tomography (CT) and positron emission tomography
scans obtained for cancer staging, which showed synovitis in a subset of patients before they presented for rheumatology evaluation.
Risk factors for developing ICI-induced IA are largely
unknown. It can occur with anti-PD-1, anti-PD-L1, or combination PD-1/CTLA-4 inhibition. It has been noted in patients
with a variety of tumor types from melanoma and NSCLC to
prostate cancer and Hodgkin’s lymphoma. A preliminary study
suggests that possessing the HLA DRB1 shared epitope allele may
be associated with developing ICI-induced IA.
Treatment for
ICI-induced IA is detailed in the following section. Importantly,
IA may persist after the cessation of ICI therapy and become
a chronic problem. In one study, almost 50% of patients with
follow-up 6 months after ICI cessation had ongoing symptoms

and/or required immunosuppression for ICI-induced IA.
Risk
factors were longer duration of ICI therapy, having two or more
other irAEs, and using combination anti-CTLA-4/PD-1 therapy.

### Polymyalgia Rheumatica/Giant Cell Arteritis

Both a polymyalgia rheumatica (PMR)–like syndrome and giant
cell arteritis (GCA) have occurred in patients on ICI therapy. Isolated PMR is the more common presentation, as with traditional
forms of PMR and GCA. Patients can present with hip and/or
shoulder pain and stiffness and most have elevated inflammatory
markers.
There has been a description of a clinical syndrome
with PMR features and smaller joint involvement of IA.
PMR
and GCA complicate treatment with both CTLA-4 and PD-1
inhibition.
Symptoms of ICI-related GCA mirror that of
traditional GCA and include headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication, and visual changes like amaurosis fugax with or without
PMR-type symptoms.
Inflammatory markers are elevated in
all confirmed cases of GCA due to ICIs, with C-reactive protein
level reported as high as 296 mg/L.
Temporal artery biopsies
have shown features consistent with GCA, including intimal proliferation, arteritis, and disruption of the elastic lamina.
Temporal artery biopsy is important if GCA is suspected as ICI therapy
may need to be discontinued.

### Sicca Syndrome

There have been reports of dry mouth and dry eyes, often severe,
occurring in the setting of ICI therapy.
Most of these
cases were negative for anti-Ro or anti-La antibodies, but, as in IA,
seropositive cases have also occurred. Dry mouth is commonly the
most prominent symptom. Biopsies of the minor salivary gland
have shown inflammation distinct from that of primary Sjögren’s
syndrome (
Fig. 130.2C
).
There has been one case with ICIrelated parotitis that responded to corticosteroids. Another case
described a sensory neuropathy in the setting of a salivary gland
biopsy with features of Sjögren’s syndrome.

### Myositis

Inflammatory myopathies due to ICIs are a rare but increasingly
appreciated adverse effect. Most cases are more similar to polymyositis
without classic skin rashes of dermatomyositis; however, several cases of dermatomyositis have occurred.
As with
PMR/GCA, cases have been seen with both CTLA-4 and PD-1/
PD-L1 blockade. Proximal muscle weakness is the most common
presentation, but distal, respiratory, and neck weakness and concomitant myocarditis have been seen. Creatine kinase levels are
typically elevated, ranging from 500 to over 16,000 U/L. Rhabdomyolysis can occur in some patients.
Myositis may also be
accompanied by fasciitis, which can be quite painful, as in traditional inflammatory myopathies.
MRI may show inflammation
of the involved muscles or fascia (
Fig. 130.2D
). Electromyography
has shown myopathic patterns, many consistent with an irritable
myopathy.
Muscle biopsies have shown primarily endomysial
inflammation, necrotizing myopathy, and only one reported case
had a dermatomyositis-like pattern.
Myositis-specific antibodies have been mostly negative.
One study showed that preexisting anti-acetylcholine receptor antibodies and lymphopenia
were associated with developing myositis after PD-1 treatment.
As with other irAEs, the precise risk factors for development of
myositis are unknown and subject to ongoing investigation.
Additionally, patients with myasthenia gravis/myositis overlap
and myocarditis/myositis overlap have been described; this is especially important to remember in patients presenting with respiratory compromise.
Mortality risk increases with either of these
overlap conditions. In one study using the World Health Organization (WHO) pharmacovigilance database, 56.7% of cases of
myositis with concurrent myocarditis were fatal, while 27.9% of
the myositis/myasthenia cases and 16% of isolated myositis were
fatal.
If myositis is suspected, further evaluation to determine
whether concomitant myasthenia or myocarditis are present is
indicated (
Fig. 130.3
).

### Other Vasculitis

There are limited descriptions of single-organ vasculitis affecting the uterus
and retina
and also of systemic anti-neutrophil
cytoplasmic antibody–associated vasculitis,
in ICI-treated
patients. Possible ICI-induced aortitis and periaortitis have also
been described in case reports.

### Scleroderma-like Disease

A small number of cases of scleroderma-like skin changes with diffuse and limited skin involvement have occurred.
One of these
cases with diffuse skin involvement had a modified Rodnan Skin
Score of 28, which improved with prednisone and mycophenolate.
In three cases where autoantibodies were tested, the patients were
negative for anti-nuclear, anti-centromere, anti-ribonucleoprotein,
anti-RNA polymerase III, and anti-topoisomerase antibodies.
There has been minimal internal organ involvement (e.g., interstitial
lung disease or pulmonary hypertension) consistent with systemic
sclerosis in ICI-induced cases described thus far, with the exception of one patient with new-onset Raynaud’s phenomenon along
with skin changes and one other patient with a scleroderma renal
crisis.
A study of the WHO pharmacovigilance database showed
disproportionality of scleroderma risk with nivolumab and pembrolizumab but not other ICIs.

### Sarcoidosis-like Disease

Rheumatologists are often called on to evaluate sarcoidosis,
particularly in suspected cases of extrapulmonary disease. Most
reported cases in ICI-induced sarcoid-like disease have both pulmonary and skin involvement. The pulmonary involvement has
included hilar and mediastinal lymphadenopathy and interstitial infiltrates (groundglass opacities, interstitial thickening).
Cytopathology of biopsied lymph nodes shows sarcoid-like granulomatous inflammation.
Skin lesions have been erythematous
papules or nodules, showing granulomatous inflammation when
biopsied.
To date, there are no reports of sarcoid arthropathy,
hepatic involvement, or other organ involvement due to ICIs.

### Others

Rheumatologists may encounter a variety of other clinical syndromes in ICI-treated patients. Eosinophilic fasciitis, characterized by skin thickening of the arms, myalgia, fascial edema on
MRI, and peripheral eosinophilia, can occur in patients treated
with anti-PD-1/PD-L1 or anti-CTLA-4 agents.
There are
several reports of remitting seronegative symmetric synovitis
with pitting edema in patients treated with ICIs.
Accelerated osteoporosis leading to compression fractures and focal bone

patients.
Additionally, an acral vascular syndrome, similar to
critical digital ischemia in scleroderma, has been described in a
patient treated with ICIs.
Interestingly, there remain only a few case reports of lupus
nephritis occurring due to ICI therapy.
Subacute cutaneous lupus has been described more extensively but without clear
accompanying systemic symptoms.
The absence of systemic
lupus erythematous (SLE) from rheumatologic irAEs is intriguing
and may speak to key differences in pathogenesis between lupus
and other rheumatologic diseases.

## Treatment of Rheumatologic Immune- Related Adverse Events

### General Treatment Principles

For oncologists, management is typically guided by the severity of
the irAE, as defined by the National Cancer Institute Common Terminology Criteria for Adverse Events.
Generally, grade 1 events
are mild, grade 2 events are moderate and can affect instrumental
activities of daily living, grade 3 events are severe and disabling (affecting self-care), and grade 4 events are life threatening. For grade 3 or
higher events, ICIs are universally held and often discontinued.
The American Society of Clinical Oncologists, the National
Comprehensive Cancer Network, and the Society for Immunotherapy of Cancer have issued guidelines for the management of a
variety of irAEs, including rheumatic irAEs.
Relevant guidelines for rheumatologists include IA, polymyalgia-like syndrome,
and myositis/myalgia.
The initial treatment for moderate to severe irAEs in general
(not specifically for rheumatic irAEs) is corticosteroids at doses
ranging from 0.5 to 2 mg/kg. In those who do not respond to or

> Myasthenia gravis

who are intolerant to corticosteroids, there should be consideration
of other types of immunosuppression. The main classes of immunosuppression/modulation that may be considered in treating
rheumatologic irAEs, their potential risks and benefits, and their
prior use for treatment of irAEs are summarized in
Table 130.2
.

### Inflammatory Arthritis Treatment

As the treatment of ICI-induced IA has been best described for
rheumatologic irAEs, it can serve as an example for thinking about
treatment across the spectrum of severity and persistence. For mild
cases of IA, where symptoms do not affect activities of daily living,
scheduled nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs, topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory gel, or intra-articular steroids can be used.
These can all be continued while the patient receives ICI therapy.
For moderate cases of IA, corticosteroids initially at lower doses
than used in other types of irAEs can be trialed. Some patients
will respond to 20 mg of prednisone daily, as in traditional forms
of IA, but others will need much higher doses, up to 1 mg/kg. If a
patient is on less than 10 to 20 mg daily of daily prednisone, many
oncologists will often continue the ICI rather than hold it. If the
patient requires higher steroid doses, the ICI is typically held. If
steroids cannot be tapered to a low dose or discontinued in 4 to 6
weeks, then other immunosuppressive agents can be trialed. The
choice of agent depends on the severity and clinical features of the
arthritis, any other irAEs the patient has currently or had previously, and plans for future ICI use/other cancer treatments. For
example, if the patient has a highly inflammatory oligoarthritis
with prior colitis and the oncologist would like to rechallenge with
an ICI, it may be best to give a short course of TNF inhibition to
control the arthritis. Tocilizumab has also been used successfully
to treat patients with ICI-induced IA. There is now some experience in giving TNF inhibitors for colitis and IL-6R inhibitors for
IA while continuing ICI therapy.
If the patient is still having

with methotrexate or leflunomide for moderate cases or hydroxychloroquine or sulfasalazine for more mild cases.

### Myositis

The treatment for ICI-related myositis depends primarily on its
severity and the presence of concomitant irAEs. The starting dose
of corticosteroids depends on whether the patient is hospitalized, whether there is concomitant myasthenia or myocarditis,
and whether there is diaphragmatic dysfunction or dysphagia. In
patients who are hospitalized with severe symptoms, an initial pulse
dose of 1000 mg intravenous (IV) methylprednisolone is often
used with subsequent tapering, whereas outpatients can be treated
with initial prednisone doses of 1 mg/kg/day dosing. In severe or
refractory cases of myositis, IV immunoglobulin
has been
used, and plasmapheresis
has been attempted with limited
success. This is especially relevant for cases with respiratory compromise. With myasthenia overlap, pyridostigmine has been used
in addition to immunosuppression.
If myocarditis is present,
there are case reports of giving abatacept or mycophenolate.

### Specific Treatments in Other Syndromes

In sicca syndrome, treatments have been primarily symptomatic,
focusing on dry eyes and dry mouth. Saliva substitutes and sialagogues such as pilocarpine and cevimeline are used for dry mouth
and artificial tears or punctal plugs can be used for dry eyes. It is
important for these patients to see their dentist and ophthalmologist regularly. There is also a suggestion that prednisone can be
used in severe cases of dry mouth from sicca syndrome to improve
salivary function.
Prednisone can be started at 20 to 40 mg daily
and tapered over 4 to 6 weeks.
In GCA, IV corticosteroids are often used initially, at initial
doses as high as 1000 mg daily of methylprednisolone. ICIs have
been held in all reported cases to date.

### Concerns With Immunosuppression

There are theoretical concerns that treating irAEs with immunosuppression will negatively impact the tumor response. This does
not seem to be the case for short-term therapy with corticosteroids or TNF inhibitors for irAEs in melanoma patients treated
with ipilimumab or nivolumab.
In fact, there is now interest in how cytokine blockade combined with ICI therapy may
be beneficial to tumor response.
There is emerging evidence,
however, that the use of corticosteroids at the onset of ICI therapy
may be detrimental. In a study of patients being treated with antiPD-1 and anti-PD-L1 agents for NSCLC, baseline use of 10 mg
of prednisone or greater daily was associated with worsened overall response rate, progression-free survival, and overall survival.
Studies evaluating the long-term durability of response and the
effects of immunosuppression are lacking. This is particularly relevant for rheumatic irAEs as more long-term immunosuppression
may be required to treat them due to persistence of the rheumatic
phenotype.

### Multidisciplinary Care

Successful treatment of rheumatologic irAEs requires collaboration among the oncology care team, the rheumatologist, and any
other specialists involved (in the case of multiple irAEs). There is
no consensus about the duration of ICI therapy required for sustained response within or across tumor types. Thus the patient’s
oncologist may have particular goals in terms of duration of therapy that impact the rheumatology treatment plan. Timely and
direct communication between the rheumatologist and oncologist
can allow therapy to be tailored to the patient’s unique clinical
scenario.

## Pre-existing Autoimmune Disease and Immune Checkpoint Inhibitor Use

The association between autoimmunity and cancer has been
appreciated in a variety of rheumatic diseases.
Though patients
with autoimmune disease are often at increased risk of certain
cancers,
they were excluded from most of the early clinical
trials for ICIs. Thus patients with diseases like RA or SLE and
advanced-stage cancer have received ICIs primarily as standard of
care. As a result, there is a lack of prospectively collected data on
outcomes and safety of ICIs in this population. Data from the
published literature, primarily from retrospective studies, are summarized here.
The first retrospective studies of patients with pre-existing
autoimmune disease were from melanoma, one with 30 patients
treated with ipilimumab
and another involving 52 patients
treated with anti-PD-1 agents.
These studies grouped all
autoimmune diseases together and included patients with RA,
inflammatory bowel disease, psoriasis, SLE, multiple sclerosis,
polymyalgia rheumatica, and a variety of other conditions. Flare
rates of the pre-existing autoimmune diseases in these studies were
27% and 38%, respectively, while grade 3 or higher de novo irAEs
occurred in 33% of those treated with ipilimumab and 21% in
those treated with anti-PD-1 agents.
A systematic literature review of retrospective studies and
case reports included 123 patients with pre-existing autoimmunity.
In this study, 75% had an exacerbation of their preexisting rheumatic disease, 25% had a de novo irAE, but only
17.1% had to discontinue ICI therapy due to flare or irAE. In
general, the rate of exacerbations was higher with PD-1/PD-L1
inhibition, whereas the rate of de novo irAEs was higher with
CTLA-4 inhibition. The largest group of patients with rheumatologic disease had RA (
N
= 20); 35% had an arthritis flare only,
25% had a de novo irAE, and 15% had both. Larger systematic
literature reviews have since been published showing similar
tolerability. A systematic review from 2021 with 512 patients
showed 44% of patients experienced a flare of their underlying
autoimmune disease but only 18.2% discontinued ICI therapy
due to immunotoxicity.

### Concurrent Immunosuppression

One question asked of rheumatologists by oncologists is whether
to stop or continue immunosuppression in a patient with preexisting autoimmune disease when they are starting ICI treatment
for cancer. In a series of lung cancer patients with pre-existing
autoimmune disease, 20% were receiving immunomodulatory
agents at the start of ICI treatment, including corticosteroids,
hydroxychloroquine, tofacitinib, mesalamine, sulfasalazine, or
apremilast.
In a series of melanoma patients with pre-existing
autoimmune disease treated with PD-1 inhibition, 37% were
on corticosteroids and/or steroid-sparing agents (leflunomide,

hydroxychloroquine, apremilast, methotrexate, sulfasalazine, or
mesalamine). Some potentially concerning data comes from a
national French study on patients with pre-existing autoimmune
disease, some of whom were on immunosuppression at the start of
ICI therapy and others who were not.
In the group of patients
on immunosuppression at baseline, there was significantly
decreased progression-free survival compared with those patients
with autoimmune disease not on baseline immunosuppression.
Information is still not complete on which immunomodulatory
medications affect tumor response to ICIs due to the relatively
small numbers of patients and the heterogeneity of underlying
rheumatic diseases, immunomodulatory treatment, cancer type,
and ICI regimen. Timing of the immunosuppression likely matters as the studies showing a negative effect on tumor response
examined the impact of immunosuppression at the time of ICI
treatment initiation. This includes the aforementioned national
French study of patients with pre-existing autoimmune disease
and also a study of lung cancer patients where those on 10 mg
of daily prednisone or higher at baseline had worsened tumor
response to ICIs.
Though there are limited data regarding use of ICIs in
patients with autoimmune disease, they seem to be generally well tolerated, and flares rarely lead to discontinuation of
therapy. To manage patients with pre-existing autoimmune
disease and advanced cancer on ICI therapy, multidisciplinary
care and communication are key. Educating oncologists about
the signs and symptoms of flare and if any laboratory tests are
required for serial monitoring, and seeing patients more frequently while they are on ICI therapy, can help ensure that
flares are diagnosed and treated.

Full references for this chapter can be found on Elsevier eBooks+.

Chapter 1
:
We are grateful to our colleagues Drs. Danielle Rux
and Eiki Koyama for providing images of joint tissues and helping
in the preparation of schematics. Original studies in the authors’
laboratories were supported from grants from the National Institutes of Health.

Chapter 7
:
The authors thank Professor Frank P. Luyten for his
contributions to the previous versions of this chapter.

Chapter 15
:
The authors’ studies have been supported by National
Institutes of Health grants, the United States-Israel Binational
Science Foundation, and the Department of Pathology, Stanford
University School of Medicine.

Chapter 21
:
This work was supported by National Institutes of
Health grants from the National Institute of Arthritis and Musculoskeletal and Skin Diseases, the National Institute of Allergy and
Infectious Diseases, and the National Institute of Environmental
Health Sciences.

Chapter 98
:
We acknowledge the excellent assistance of Mary
Grace Whelan in preparation of the CPPD portions of this
chapter.
Chapter 104
:
We wish to acknowledge Prof. Reuven Mader, a
great physician and mentor for generations of Israeli rheumatologists, who passed away during the work on Chapter 104. Reuven
led the EULAR group of DISH for the past decade and paved the
way for future insights into rheumatic diseases.

Chapter 119
:
This chapter is dedicated to the life and legacy of
Dr. Philip Robinson, MBChB, PhD, FRACP, who passed away at
the age of 43 in early January 2023. Phil was an associate professor
in the faculty of medicine at the Royal Brisbane Clinical Unit at
the University of Queensland in Australia. He also co-founded the
COVID-19 GRA and chaired its Steering Committee. Phil’s profound impact on our understanding of COVID-19 on patients
with rheumatic disease was driven by his inclusivity, enthusiasm,
and visionary leadership. Equally important, his influence on the
many rheumatologists who had their early careers dramatically
change through his extraordinary mentorship and sponsorship
through the GRA will be felt for decades to come. Phil was a very
close colleague and an even closer friend to the authors of this
chapter.
Vale Phil.
